{"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_01KsFYs3Nz57YEnVeqk9WwjP","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"thinking","thinking":"So `azx_stop_chip` does clear `chip->initialized = 0`. That means `azx_init_chip` should work properly when called from `azx_resume`. But wait - let me look at `azx_bus_reset` more carefully:\n\n```c\nstatic void azx_bus_reset(struct hda_bus *bus)\n{\n\tstruct azx *chip = bus->private_data;\n\n\tbus->in_reset = 1;\n\tazx_stop_chip(chip);\n\tazx_init_chip(chip, 1);\n```\n\nHere, `azx_stop_chip` sets `chip->initialized = 0`, and then `azx_init_chip` will proceed. \n\nThat seems fine. But I'm wondering if the real problem is that `polling_mode` and `single_cmd` flags persist across suspend cycles. When a timeout occurs during one suspend-resume cycle, the driver falls back to these modes, but they don't get cleared when resuming again. So after multiple suspends, these flags remain set from earlier failures, causing the azx_get_response timeout to keep happening.\n\nLooking at the initialization flow, `azx_init_chip` checks if the chip is already initialized and returns early, while `azx_stop_chip` resets the initialized flag. But the real issue is that the timeout happens immediately after resume, not during playback, which suggests the problem occurs during the reset sequence itself rather than later on.\n\nThe error {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_01J1HoLURrdxusqXZ6fXwK3G","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"thinking","thinking":"I notice a potential bug in `azx_reset` at line 1104-1107:\n\n```c\n/* Accept unsolicited responses */\nif (!chip->single_cmd)\n    azx_writel(chip, GCTL, azx_readl(chip, GCTL) |\n               ICH6_GCTL_UNSOL);\n```\n\nThis only accepts unsolicited responses if `!chip->single_cmd`. But if the system is recovering from a timeout (where `chip->single_cmd` was set to 1 due to a timeout), then `single_cmd` will be 1 and unsolicited responses won't be enabled.\n\n\nThe real problem is that when `azx_init_chip` runs after a timeout, it skips reinitializing the CORB/RIRB if `chip->single_cmd` is still set to 1, leaving the system permanently stuck in single command mode. This explains the choppy audio—the hardware never recovers from the timeout state.\n\nThe fix would be to reset `chip->single_cmd` back to 0 on resume (unless it was explicitly set via module parameter), allowing the CORB/RIRB to reinitialize properly. Looking at `azx_init_chip`:\n\n```c\nstatic void azx_init_chip(struct azx *chip, int full_reset)\n{\n    if (chip->initialized)\n        return;\n\n    /* reset controller */\n    azx_reset(chip, full_reset);\n\n    /* initialize interrupts */\n    azx_int_clear(chip);\n    azx_int_enable(chip);\n\n    /* initialize the codec command I/O */\n    if (!chip->single_cmd)\n        azx_init_cmd_io(chip);\n\n    /* program the position buffer */\n    azx_writel(chip, DPLBASE, (u32)chip->posbuf.addr);\n    azx_writel(chip, DPUBASE, upper_32_bits(chip->posbuf.addr));\n\n    chip->initialized = 1;\n}\n```\n\nThe problem is that in `azx_resume`, these flags aren't being reset, so if a timeout previously forced `chip->single_cmd = 1`, that state persists on the next resume instead of being reinitialized.\n\nLooking at the actual sequence though, the real puzzle is what triggers that initial timeout. The key insight is that `chip->poll_count` never resets between suspend/resume cycles. If it reaches 2 or higher, the system skips the polling fallback entirely and goes straight to single_cmd mode. So after a few suspend/resume cycles where timeouts occur, the accumulated poll_count pushes the system into this degraded state permanently. I see the poll_count accumulates across operations when polling succeeds without resetting, which could explain why subsequent timeouts jump straight to polling mode. But I'm not convinced that's the core issue—the real question is why the codec stops responding to commands after a resume in the first place. Let me trace through the suspend sequence more carefully: the driver sends a power-down command during suspend, then on resume it needs to restore the codec state, but something in that handshake is breaking. But when resuming, the code always sends D0 power state commands to wake the codec back up, regardless of whether it was explicitly suspended.\n\nLooking at the bigger picture, `snd_hda_resume` unconditionally calls `hda_call_codec_resume` for all codecs, and there's potential overlap with `__snd_hda_power_up` which also triggers codec resume. The real issue seems to be that `snd_hda_suspend` only suspends codecs that are actually powered on, so there's an asymmetry between what gets suspended and what gets resumed during system sleep cycles. The `snd_hda_power_down` call at the end decrements the `power_count` back to its previous value, so if it was 0 before resume, it goes back to 0 and schedules the power-down work—which should be fine for system resume. But I'm noticing the intermittent nature and the \"after a few suspends\" pattern suggests state isn't being properly reset somewhere, and I'm suspecting `poll_count` might not be getting cleared as it should.\n\nLooking more closely at the chip initialization and shutdown, when `azx_stop_chip` runs during suspend it marks the chip as uninitialized, and `azx_init_chip` during resume calls `azx_reset` with a full reset. The reset should clear the command buffer through `azx_init_cmd_io`, but I'm wondering if there's an issue with the controller reset itself not completing properly or leaving stale state behind.\n\nThen there's the runtime PM interaction to consider. If the codec gets powered down by runtime PM before system suspend, the suspend path skips calling `hda_call_codec_suspend` since the codec is already off. Resume then reinitializes everything through `azx_init_chip` and calls `hda_call_codec_resume`. This should work, but I'm concerned about whether there could be a pending power-down work scheduled that interferes with the resume sequence.\n\nLooking at the suspend function, it cancels any pending power work, but only if the codec is still powered on. And if `single_cmd` gets set to 1, it stays at 1 permanently until reboot.\n\n\nNow I'm wondering when `polling_mode` actually gets set to 1 during the resume sequence. Looking at the reset path, the controller gets fully reset and there's a 1ms wait for the codecs to initialize, but then `snd_hda_resume` is called immediately after. The timing might be the issue here—maybe the codec isn't ready yet when the first command gets sent.\n\nBut what's interesting is that this problem only appears \"after a few\" suspends, not on the first one. I think the `poll_count` accumulation across multiple suspend/resume cycles could be the culprit. Each time the codec is slow to respond during resume, `poll_count` increments, and once it reaches 2, the next timeout triggers `polling_mode = 1`, which then cascades into disabling MSI and setting `single_cmd = 1`.\n\nThe key insight is that `poll_count` doesn't reset unless a response succeeds without polling already being enabled. So if polling gets triggered once and succeeds, `poll_count` stays elevated for the next resume cycle, making it easier to hit the threshold that enables polling mode permanently. So when a command is retried with polling after a timeout, `poll_count` stays elevated at 1 unless the next command execution resets it back to 0 on success. The key is that `poll_count` only accumulates if we keep hitting the polling path—once we return to normal execution without polling, it gets cleared.\n\nBut if the next command also times out while `poll_count` is already at 1, we increment to 2, and at that threshold the system switches into full polling mode rather than continuing with the timeout-and-retry pattern. This means we need multiple consecutive timeouts across different commands to trigger the mode switch.\n\nThe real issue is that `poll_count` never gets reset to 0 during chip initialization or reset on resume, so any polling state from a previous suspend persists into the next one. {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_01UQ7E6ps54bETK5M5ivK3aV","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"thinking","thinking":"Confirmed: `poll_count` is only reset to 0 when `do_poll = 0` and the response is received successfully. There's no reset in `azx_init_chip`, `azx_reset`, or `azx_stop_chip`.\n\nSo the bug might be:\n1. After a suspend/resume cycle, the codec is slow to respond to the first command, causing a timeout. `poll_count` gets incremented to 1 (due to `do_poll` retry). If the poll retry succeeds, `poll_count` remains at 1 because it's only reset when `do_poll = 0`.\n\n\n2. Subsequent successful commands reset `poll_count` to 0, but if another slow response occurs during the same resume, `poll_count` could climb higher.\n3. On the next resume, if `poll_count` is already elevated from the previous session, any timeout immediately triggers `polling_mode = 1`, which cascades into MSI being disabled and eventually `single_cmd = 1`.\n\nThe key question is whether `poll_count` persists across suspend/resume cycles or gets cleared during `snd_hda_resume`.\n\nThe regression from 3.6.11 to 3.7.1 is the real clue here. Something changed in that version that's causing this behavior. Looking at the resume path, `azx_init_chip` is called with a full reset flag, which should reinitialize the controller completely.\n\nNow I'm examining the initialization sequence more carefully. The function resets the controller, clears and enables interrupts, initializes the command I/O interface, and sets up the position buffer. The issue might be in how these steps interact during resume, or perhaps there's a timing problem with codec communication after the reset. , during `azx_init_chip` (or better, during resume), these dynamically-set flags should be reset so the driver can attempt normal operation again instead of staying locked in degraded modes.\n\nThe tricky part is distinguishing between `single_cmd` set as a module parameter versus set dynamically due to a timeout—there's no flag tracking which case caused it. Since timeouts are the typical trigger rather than explicit module parameters, resetting it on resume seems like the right approach, though I need to think through whether this could break cases where someone intentionally set it as a parameter. 0`. Maybe also reset `chip->single_cmd = 0` if it wasn't set as a module parameter.\n\nI'm still uncertain about the exact source of the bug, so let me look for any known fixes related to this issue. I should also examine the `snd_hda_suspend` function more carefully—specifically how it handles codec power states. If a codec was powered down by runtime PM, the suspend might skip calling the codec's suspend function, but then on resume `snd_hda_resume` sends D0 commands, which should theoretically work. Let me trace through both the suspend and resume paths to see what's actually happening.\n\nLooking at the suspend logic, it only explicitly suspends codecs that are currently powered on. If runtime PM has already powered down a codec, it gets skipped during suspend. Then on resume, the function calls the codec resume for all codecs regardless of their power state. This asymmetry might be where the issue lies.\n\nExamining the resume path... Tracing through the power management flow: `hda_keep_power_on` increments the power count and sets the flag, then `snd_hda_power_down` decrements it back. With the count at zero but the power flag still on, the system schedules a delayed power-down work if the power save timeout is configured.\n\nI'm wondering if the real issue is that `hda_call_codec_resume` gets invoked even when the codec was never explicitly suspended during the initial suspend call—it might have already been in a runtime PM-powered-off state. So on resume, the codec receives D0 commands, but maybe there's a state mismatch or the codec needs something else to be properly initialized first.\n\nLet me trace through the actual sequence: `azx_init_chip` runs before `snd_hda_resume`, which then calls down through `hda_call_codec_resume` → `hda_set_power_state` → `snd_hda_codec_read` → `hda_codec_exec_verb` → `snd_hda_power_up`. That power-up call triggers a chain that might be racing with or depending on the hardware initialization state.\n\nSince `hda_keep_power_on` was already set to 1 in `hda_call_codec_resume`, the `__snd_hda_power_up` call will just return early without doing anything else. So that's not the issue. Let me shift focus and look at the runtime PM handling in `azx_resume` and `azx_suspend` themselves—maybe something changed in how 3.7 handles `pm_runtime_get_noresume` or `pm_runtime_put_autosuspend` that's causing the {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_0193rsuZJUvJ4au5wGSC2Kjn","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"thinking","thinking":"So Intel 5 Series/3400 (0x3b56) uses `AZX_DRIVER_SCH | AZX_DCAPS_INTEL_PCH`, which includes `AZX_DCAPS_PM_RUNTIME`. This means runtime PM is enabled for this device.\n\nRuntime PM means the HDA controller can be powered down when not in use. The `azx_power_notify` function handles this via `pm_runtime_get_sync`/`pm_runtime_put_sync`.\n\nNow here's a critical point: when the system suspends, if runtime PM had already powered down the controller (`azx_runtime_suspend` was called), then the system suspend's `azx_suspend` also calls `azx_stop_chip`. But wait, `azx_stop_chip` checks `if (!chip->initialized)`. \n\nIf runtime PM already called `azx_stop_chip`, then `chip->initialized = 0`, and the system suspend's `azx_stop_chip` will be a no-op. Then on system resume, `azx_resume` calls `azx_init_chip(chip, 1)`, which will reset and reinitialize the chip since `chip->initialized = 0`. The real problem might be a race condition where system suspend happens while runtime PM resume is in progress or just completed, creating a conflict between the two power management paths.\n\nLooking at the runtime PM setup, the device has `AZX_DCAPS_PM_RUNTIME` enabled, but `azx_suspend` doesn't call `pm_runtime_disable` and `azx_resume` doesn't call `pm_runtime_enable`. The `azx_power_notify` function calls `pm_runtime_get_sync`/`pm_runtime_put_sync` when the codec is powered up/down.\n\nThe `power_save_controller` module parameter defaults to `1` (true), and `azx_runtime_idle` allows idle if `power_save_controller` is true and the driver caps include `AZX_DCAPS_PM_RUNTIME`. So for the 5 Series/3400, the runtime PM idle/suspend IS enabled.\n\n\nWhen no codec is active, the power save mechanism kicks in and powers down the codecs through a chain that eventually calls `pm_runtime_put_sync`, triggering the runtime suspend handler which stops the chip and marks it as uninitialized. Then during system suspend, the driver calls `snd_hda_suspend` on the bus, but there's a question about what state the codec is in at that point.\n\nOn resume, the driver reinitializes the PCI device, resets the chip since it was marked uninitialized, and then resumes all the codecs. But I'm wondering what happens after the codec resume completes—specifically whether the power state gets properly restored. I'm tracing through the resume sequence more carefully now. After `hda_call_codec_resume` completes and `codec->in_pm` is cleared, `snd_hda_power_down` schedules `hda_power_work` to potentially suspend the codec again if nothing's using it. The system then calls `snd_power_change_state` to wake sleeping processes and resume normal operation, but I'm not seeing an obvious problem in this flow. Let me shift focus to the runtime PM interaction with system suspend—specifically what happens when runtime PM has already suspended the controller and then system suspend kicks in.\n\nDuring system suspend, `azx_suspend` essentially becomes a no-op since the codecs are already runtime-PM-suspended and the chip is already stopped. On resume, the sequence reinitializes the PCI device, restores MSI and IRQ handling, and then performs a full chip initialization since the initialized flag was cleared.\n\nThe problem emerges when system suspend happens right after runtime PM suspension—the IRQ was never freed and MSI was never disabled during runtime suspend, only during the full system suspend. When the system resumes, `pci_restore_state` brings back the MSI state, but then `azx_acquire_irq` tries to acquire the IRQ again, which could cause conflicts or unexpected behavior. So the IRQ stays valid during runtime PM since it's never freed, but I'm seeing a potential race condition where a system suspend could trigger codec suspension and stop the chip while runtime PM thinks the device is still active. Let me trace through what happens in `snd_hda_resume` to see if there's something breaking the power state tracking. Looking at the suspend path more carefully, when `hda_call_codec_suspend` is called during system suspend (not from the work queue), it sets the codec to D3 power state, cleans up streams, and then cancels any pending power work before updating the power accounting. When the codec resumes, if it was previously powered down, the code calls `pm_runtime_get_sync` to wake the chip back up, which triggers `azx_runtime_resume` to restart the hardware.\n\nThe issue is that `azx_stop_chip` disables the CORB/RIRB DMA during suspend but doesn't fully reset their state, and while `azx_init_chip` will reinitialize when the chip resumes, there might be a mismatch in how the command buffers are handled between the suspend and resume paths.\n\nNow I'm looking at the codec suspend/resume logic to see if there's a problem with codecs that are already runtime-PM-suspended not being properly handled during the bus-level suspend operation.\n\nExamining the resume function...\n\nSo `hda_call_codec_resume` runs through all codecs and resumes them, but then the power management logic kicks in—it increments the power count, calls the resume handler, and then decrements it back. If the power save timeout is active, the codec gets scheduled to power down again after the delay, which means it goes through the full power-down sequence including the codec suspend callback.\n\nThe race condition I'm worried about is if this delayed power-down happens while the system is already suspending again, but mutexes should protect against that. This means the codecs never get explicitly transitioned to D3 state, so when resume tries to bring them back to D0, they're in an unexpected state that causes the communication timeouts.\n\nBut wait—the hardware reset that happens during resume should put the codecs back into their default D0 state according to the HDA spec, so resending D0 commands should be harmless. So the verb is AC_VERB_SET_POWER_STATE and the data is 0x03, meaning this command is putting the AFG node into D3 power state during suspend. The AFG (Audio Function Group) at NID 0x01 is the right target for power state commands. Looking at the resume flow, `hda_call_codec_resume` sets D0 power state, then calls `snd_hda_power_down` which asynchronously schedules a power work function that eventually sends the D3 command after a delay. So the timeout error showing the D3 command as the last command suggests the codec isn't responding to that power-down request during or shortly after resume. During resume, the codec initialization function sends a D0 power state command, but if a D3 command was pending from before suspend, that stale command might still be in the buffer causing the timeout. The issue could be that the first command during resume isn't clearing out this old state properly, leaving the codec unresponsive. So the D3 command gets sent during suspend but the RIRB response never arrives—maybe a timing issue. The command stays in `chip->last_cmd[0]` as `0x00170503`, suspend continues without error checking, and then after resume the RIRB reinitializes. Though actually, a response from before the hardware reset shouldn't come back after that. Let me look more closely at how `azx_rirb_get_response` handles the timeout logic.\n\nThe function polls `chip->rirb.cmds[addr]` in a loop, waiting for it to hit zero—that counter gets incremented when a command is sent and decremented when the response arrives. If the timeout expires before the response comes back, the function breaks out of the loop.\n\nNow I'm thinking through a potential race condition: what if a command was sent before suspend, but the response never got properly counted? Then after resume, that leftover counter value would still be sitting there, and the next command would need two responses instead of one, causing an immediate timeout. Let me trace through how this could happen—a command gets sent (counter becomes 1), the system suspends before the response comes back, and then `azx_free_cmd_io` gets called during the stop sequence, which shuts down the CORB/RIRB hardware.\n\nBut wait, when `azx_init_cmd_io` runs during resume, it calls `memset` on the command counter array, resetting everything to zero. So that's not the issue.\n\nLet me look at the controller state itself after suspend/resume—specifically how the CORB read pointer gets reset and the CORB DMA gets re-enabled.\n\nI'm noticing something: the reset bit for CORBRP gets set, but I need to check if it's being cleared afterward. According to the HDA spec, you have to write 1 to reset the pointer, then write 0 to clear that reset bit. If the driver only sets it without clearing it, the CORB DMA won't function properly after resume.","signature":"EvVeCkYICxgCKkCu/suVgpHncfgFsieszp3/LwjxMPvmtaI9v0V958qCQw92HCj6k4BnzArZN/DeT3bgEFQixh3CSSmukDykRMnYEgzBfoELG/o5shh/BsUaDAdHC746rTXDdyJ8+SIwBTOGTTv/K7X6N7IAgZSX9I0ziCDa/SXXn47Enqzx5Zba65jLarR4RGmOfSLUxkacKtxd5Fyya1FyAESdDT07/6il9LWb23PGFmmYEvvbzkB/nNnkFAeIh1Y5JhnkMFY5LVQ9bnZ0lWeyzRsd5cKM9A7MxdqrobpPeyuOwNcDQJHTM2mhtQFJHKTr+QmbB7CAxnyQT+C3f4jJIc3bmp2YCp9S2OHpzi1SGQ8z1P/WtgA44xpFoAFXlqi/QGW9kUlVkfe1gOdB3o7BbP74uvfZratuNE96Ao3/5apy+o0PYw3SGr2+a4nBNsqu8Cs1BrWRr+kUCMGs/J3mT9p+Ec6SrXpW4gaeEtIGumpO0ZAaU82/aJsQsGNHHR+lPhw1g9TORF544gwp9/UNIxALcEyjzCJuNJtdV7RIpA8/5VL4OayD/HT5Vbr0H18s+vw6+cUsFYHAEuclhiuIb5mD4UbdPw42nyPT9+fzT5mnNJuBBIyz0+Y7Xz2GtlzQ7HaZE2UYNcJ5lZA1py+02LofzyD+/AVf2X0EakYb/50hOH90tQnGm0fZkVFqjy+VwRkA15w3ZgjTxSh3/LBMqitY1agsS6uTTnNmKBpRpTILGbXMgysQFBBcoNVy1mxP4FdYKfjbZe7yNk2xu9RL6HQl0kwdB1SvN8/HR57zUD0GDVV/GNMlQv3M77EFBueQseF9VKZcuYHnvvgnx71pHNm3NfT3TMVD/4KP/gKcROE8OQFY/S+JBDX88Fjo07uzKF+R9vJkGtwWpulu/5jFRhpcGih/DhqxLCXCzkT3zgzsdNM2P3UH7Cbstb+peSdlnQk/jmozpNw9t2NXGX9mqo9AcSliwXRhgBLZsA6e6ddWJGDIjOqChCJi7jv0bDmAXvcskh0a2734g4nWjl1SzjWfxXBFTY09jdWoO8f9N2IK8UKAQXwWZXwZDrtbI7z9Eaz+rpfkEpbFnqDEJCBm/PXZoWu71KcntmuEWCDmSWYMjR8EGB4sWLon7JQ3iYXHYVOYtlSSh/DvusxYMztkVI9K2ZfR7WnxjeWggqzSFHkQCbgP5MQC37tBt2KPDtknfGiWeWsmvjfincVuzq8PrM8y3qa5P+favLa97GQs8NaRwB/TgCqSXmJziX639XlOghcl5xUOo5cO6cvsNDd0fEv/fi2AdZFjY4XxDsnuGSp0A9OrF3NjogcFHGLWlvbaCTJyO7UpljWygbbJWz7J2dZ1aoWsWKf2XW/2u+s7ztft/+7MnaR9Ehh6oxhpurLPbEHHBewdYCaPm595LbQKmCtlAVwIOaD/C3xqyVyin/eWIMOwU9GL39mmwM5iRmHWyxwp4miboquiVb75Dg6rki3/v2gcUIzcSq+bdaVZDqPWqRohFh9QFyMbVPPfacarmZ2JrIfNBgFQzEOHDHfdJOXyMwu2xQ9vsDOx2atmIWCj0r357YJNZv0A0JcLUuS0Hy5KkbHb0O+/mTUVaX+PU63iZVvR2JXRiWXIoQHWWdoCugb/9EICBSY1FC4sRLVi1hZzPR3zL2lBD1u3Aekfr5g1TR62DqvzRfJQxXmme60+C8mbI+aRWXADyarcxSjs+MTDa6eLMnzBwukG5IGussKRKEh8Auwvf3sqkJfvxjroaqs9h+hG0snhvctSaBd7sCcHCw5CHsqVM81jZd+gQpv8tPHMtxCwhFWRA0Y0JHi1tV+3vBnkePkP4FMZXd3v93jKYXOpM6wD8pkUEy/D4cc1s1tkPz5UrBWYkoa3P+jbWvxZvnA33S+hN4FdtyfsR/Acqi/hT6yKMsUCfg+oVdswZkDQicE3SpceDYjnen4+jWgmAtl/7d Let me trace through what happens:\n\n1. The critical issue is that if `chip->single_cmd` is set to 1, the CORB/RIRB command I/O is skipped entirely during re-initialization. But after a hardware reset, those structures are cleared and need to be re-initialized regardless of the single_cmd flag.\n\nNow I'm realizing the deeper problem: when `polling_mode` is enabled from a previous timeout, the CORB/RIRB still gets initialized if `single_cmd` is 0, but the code relies on explicit polling calls rather than interrupt-driven updates. I think the core issue is that when `polling_mode` persists across suspends, it bypasses the normal interrupt path while still allowing RIRB interrupts to arrive, causing the response to be processed twice—once by the interrupt handler and again by the explicit polling call in the main loop.\n\nNow I'm realizing that if a response gets counted twice, the `rirb.cmds` counter could underflow if it's a signed integer, causing the completion check to never trigger and leading to a timeout. Similarly, if `chip->polling_mode` is still 1, the next timeout will skip normal recovery and escalate to MSI disable then single_cmd mode. The fix is to reset both flags in `azx_resume` or in `azx_init_chip` during a full reset so the device can properly recover.\n\nLooking at the actual bug more carefully, the root cause is that `azx_init_chip` doesn't reset `chip->polling_mode` when doing a full hardware reset, which allows state to accumulate across suspend/resume cycles. The hardware expects software to poll for confirmation and then write 0 to complete the reset. But `polling_mode` has no such dependency—it's purely a fallback mechanism that should be reset on every full hardware reset.\n\nThe cleanest approach is to reset `polling_mode` in `azx_resume` or at the start of `azx_init_chip` when doing a full reset, since the interrupt path should be functional again after hardware initialization. Let me write the fault localization result:\n\nThe bug is in `sound/pci/hda/hda_intel.c`. The key issue is:\n\n1. `azx_rirb_get_response` sets `chip->polling_mode = 1` and `chip->single_cmd = 1` when RIRB timeouts occur\n2. These flags persist across suspend/resume cycles - they are **never reset** in `azx_init_chip`, `azx_stop_chip`, or `azx_resume`\n3. When `azx_init_chip` is called with `full_reset=1` during `azx_resume`:\n   - Hardware is fully reset via `azx_reset(chip, 1)`\n   - But `chip->polling_mode` stays at 1 from previous session\n   - Worse: `if (!chip->single_cmd) azx_init_cmd_io(chip)` - if `single_cmd=1` was set dynamically, CORB/RIRB is never re-initialized after the hardware reset\n4. With `polling_mode=1`, any new RIRB timeout skips the normal poll-retry mechanism and jumps straight to MSI disable → `single_cmd=1`\n5. This escalation pattern matches the bug report: \"after a few suspends\" (state accumulates), and \"choppy sound with 2-second silences\" (single_cmd mode is slow and unreliable)"}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":1,"cache_creation_input_tokens":8988,"cache_read_input_tokens":102420,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":8988,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":0},"output_tokens":8,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"},"context_management":null},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"b8caa321-1f01-4931-9287-71a81bcdbdb9","uuid":"911b3e2f-2703-4228-aa3b-b006dc815e18"}
{"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_016ZbuqKffG8fGVHr6yUEK5r","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"tool_use","id":"toolu_01XnLqnsvBK8oqyvbhv2kXUi","name":"Write","input":{"file_path":"/workspace/fault_localization_result.json","content":"{\n  \"buggy_files\": [\"sound/pci/hda/hda_intel.c\"],\n  \"buggy_functions\": [\"azx_resume\", \"azx_init_chip\"],\n  \"confidence\": 0.75,\n  \"reasoning\": \"The bug is in sound/pci/hda/hda_intel.c. When azx_rirb_get_response() encounters RIRB timeout during normal operation (e.g., runtime PM power-down sending D3 to codec), it sets chip->polling_mode=1 and eventually chip->single_cmd=1. These flags are NEVER reset during suspend/resume cycles: azx_stop_chip() only clears chip->initialized, and azx_init_chip() does not reset polling_mode or single_cmd. On resume, azx_resume() calls azx_init_chip(chip, 1) which performs a full hardware reset but preserves these stale flags. With polling_mode=1 persisting from a previous session, any new RIRB timeout on the next resume skips the normal poll-count retry mechanism and escalates directly to MSI disable then single_cmd=1. With single_cmd=1, azx_init_cmd_io() is never called in azx_init_chip() (due to 'if (!chip->single_cmd)' guard), so CORB/RIRB stays uninitialized and all codec commands go through the slow single-command path, causing choppy audio. The 'after a few suspends' behavior is explained by state accumulation: polling_mode or poll_count ratchets up across resume cycles until a fatal escalation occurs. The Intel 5 Series/3400 (PCI ID 0x3b56) is assigned AZX_DCAPS_INTEL_PCH which includes AZX_DCAPS_PM_RUNTIME, enabling runtime PM that triggers additional power-down/resume sequences which can cause the RIRB timeouts. When azx_rirb_get_response() encounters RIRB timeout during normal operation (e.g., runtime PM power-down sending D3 to codec), it sets chip->polling_mode=1 and eventually chip->single_cmd=1. These flags are NEVER reset during suspend/resume cycles: azx_stop_chip() only clears chip->initialized, and azx_init_chip() does not reset polling_mode or single_cmd. On resume, azx_resume() calls azx_init_chip(chip, 1) which performs a full hardware reset but preserves these stale flags. With polling_mode=1 persisting from a previous session, any new RIRB timeout on the next resume skips the normal poll-count retry mechanism and escalates directly to MSI disable then single_cmd=1. With single_cmd=1, azx_init_cmd_io() is never called in azx_init_chip() (due to 'if (!chip->single_cmd)' guard), so CORB/RIRB stays uninitialized and all codec commands go through the slow single-command path, causing choppy audio. The 'after a few suspends' behavior is explained by state accumulation: polling_mode or poll_count ratchets up across resume cycles until a fatal escalation occurs. The Intel 5 Series/3400 (PCI ID 0x3b56) is assigned AZX_DCAPS_INTEL_PCH which includes AZX_DCAPS_PM_RUNTIME, enabling runtime PM that triggers additional power-down/resume sequences which can cause the RIRB timeouts.
